<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer862611686701209994">When Sara and Jamie discovered the seemingly ordinary artifacts, they sensed the pull of a dim and distant place. A world of mists and forests, of ancient magics, mythical beings, ageless bards...and restless evil.<br /><br />Now, with their friends and enemies alike--Blue, the biker; Keiran, the folk musician; the Inspector from the RCMP; and the mysterious Tom Hengyr--Sara and Jamie</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText862611686701209994">When Sara and Jamie discovered the seemingly ordinary artifacts, they sensed the pull of a dim and distant place. A world of mists and forests, of ancient magics, mythical beings, ageless bards...and restless evil.<br /><br />Now, with their friends and enemies alike--Blue, the biker; Keiran, the folk musician; the Inspector from the RCMP; and the mysterious Tom Hengyr--Sara and Jamie are drawn into this enchanted land through the portals of Tamson House, that sprawling downtown edifice that straddles two worlds.<br /><br />Sweeping from ancient Wales to the streets of Ottawa today, <em>Moonheart</em> will entrance you with its tale of this world and the other one at the very edge of sight...and the unforgettable people caught up in the affairs of both. A tale of music, and motorcycles, and fey folk beyond the shadows of the moon. A tale of true magic; the tale of <em>Moonheart.</em></span>

            Shelves:

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer29809945">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2835347961901954152">5.0 stars. Fresh, original urban fantasy with deep, rich settings, well drawn characters and a terrific multi-layered plot. De Lint's use of the Celtic and North American Indian mythologies as the foundation of the story made it all the more enjoyable as they are not often portrayed in fantasy. There are so many memorable characters that it is hard to pinpoint any one that stands out above the rest, but characters like Pukwudji and Tamson House itself showed De Lint's incredible talent for creat</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2835347961901954152">5.0 stars. Fresh, original urban fantasy with deep, rich settings, well drawn characters and a terrific multi-layered plot. De Lint's use of the Celtic and North American Indian mythologies as the foundation of the story made it all the more enjoyable as they are not often portrayed in fantasy. There are so many memorable characters that it is hard to pinpoint any one that stands out above the rest, but characters like Pukwudji and Tamson House itself showed De Lint's incredible talent for creating interesting charcters. De Lint is a true talent. <br /><br />This was my first exposure to de Lint and I will certainly be reading much more of his work in the near future. </span>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/975958" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/66444-jeffrey" class="left imgcol" title="Jeffrey"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" alt="Jeffrey" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/975958?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 04, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/66444-jeffrey" class="user" title="Jeffrey">Jeffrey</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/186431.Moonheart" title="Hardcover 9781596060210" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer975958">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13121748202990448368"> With a title like Moonheart and a description that includes the phrase "fey folk beyond the shadows of the moon" you are fairly warned that this is fantasy of the uber-nerdy species. Nonetheless, this book is good fun. The characters are well-developed and the Otherworld that they enter is very romantic and captivating. What develops as the plot is good versus evil with the simple twist that the good characters are normal people like us that have no idea what they are doing and must learn how t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13121748202990448368"> With a title like Moonheart and a description that includes the phrase "fey folk beyond the shadows of the moon" you are fairly warned that this is fantasy of the uber-nerdy species. Nonetheless, this book is good fun. The characters are well-developed and the Otherworld that they enter is very romantic and captivating. What develops as the plot is good versus evil with the simple twist that the good characters are normal people like us that have no idea what they are doing and must learn how to use a whole host of new abilities they did not know they have. Perhaps the creators of the Matrix read this book. And the evil character is the physical manifestation of one good man's lust for revenge. This ranked as the coolest idea in the book for me. The other nice idea is that the magical world still exists but modern Western man rarely notices it and since he destroyed most people from the original ancient world (Think Native Americans, Mayans, Egyptians, etc.) it is slowly fading from our planet. De Lint may be a little clunky in the narrative of his characters' thoughts, a little dry in his setting descriptions, and a little corny in his random name-dropping of folk musicians (We get it! You are also a folk musician. Congratulations.) but he is a very good writer of fast-paced action. This and the development of a couple of interesting ideas make Moonheart much better than its blurb (and the fact that it was out of print for awhile) would suggest. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13121748202990448368">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_41650616">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/41650616" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/371225-rachel" class="left imgcol" title="Rachel"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1189661405p2/371225.jpg" alt="Rachel" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/41650616?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 02, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/371225-rachel" class="user" title="Rachel">Rachel</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/371225-rachel?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer41650616">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6075246353253240043">This is a very early Charles de Lint book, about a modern-day magic house, a druid, a Celtic bard, Native Americans, elves, wizards, um, am I forgetting anything? :) It's fun, imaginative, and exciting, and its only real flaw is that the characters are more like caricatures. The Native Americans are humorless native warriors who live in teepees but have a strong tradition of association with the spirits, the Celtic bard talks like, well, a Celtic bard from legend, etc.. And, like his other early</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6075246353253240043">This is a very early Charles de Lint book, about a modern-day magic house, a druid, a Celtic bard, Native Americans, elves, wizards, um, am I forgetting anything? :) It's fun, imaginative, and exciting, and its only real flaw is that the characters are more like caricatures. The Native Americans are humorless native warriors who live in teepees but have a strong tradition of association with the spirits, the Celtic bard talks like, well, a Celtic bard from legend, etc.. And, like his other early books, I feel like it pushes his magickal learning and agenda a bit too hard. But these are all relatively minor complaints, and it's a fast-paced and fun story that I really enjoyed. And I was deeply dubious about the idea of Celts coming over to America in pre-history, but it turns out there was probably a weird influx of Irish monks around 600 AD, who lived with and traded ideas with the Native Americans. Who knew?<br /><br />Anyway, fun read, and my kids and I were fascinated by the magic house. :)</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6075246353253240043">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/903381756?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 13, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6247910-casceil" class="user" title="Casceil">Casceil</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a href="/review/list/6247910-casceil?shelf=ylto-chunkster" class="actionLinkLite">ylto-chunkster</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer903381756">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17648869585275211254">I just reread this fantasy classic for the first time in twenty years. I had forgotten how much I liked it. Sarah Tams lives with her uncle, Jesse Tams, at a large and mysterious house that occupies an entire city block in Ottawa. Sarah also runs an "antiquarian" store, where they sell all sorts of odd objects. Going through storage boxes in the back room, Sarah finds some odd but intriguing items, including what appears to be a ball of clay but turns out to have a golden ring hidden inside. Fro</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17648869585275211254">I just reread this fantasy classic for the first time in twenty years. I had forgotten how much I liked it. Sarah Tams lives with her uncle, Jesse Tams, at a large and mysterious house that occupies an entire city block in Ottawa. Sarah also runs an "antiquarian" store, where they sell all sorts of odd objects. Going through storage boxes in the back room, Sarah finds some odd but intriguing items, including what appears to be a ball of clay but turns out to have a golden ring hidden inside. From that day on, strange things begin happening, and soon Sarah finds herself in Otherworld, a forested land where magic operates on certain fairly clear rules, and Sarah must learn how to use her ring to defeat an entity who is evil incarnate. While she is in Otherworld learning, Tamson House is under seige from a magical attack. It's a good thing the house is very protective of its residents. The author juggles several different story lines featuring a number of very likeable characters, all of whom are in big trouble. I thoroughly enjoyed this book, and have already purchased the sequel for my Kindle. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17648869585275211254">...more</a>

              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer15226966">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2117790675701065296">The book which was my introduction to Charles de Lint. One of the most unforgettable characters in this book is actually a house. Tamson House. A huge rooming house with a center courtyard/garden which opens into the Otherworld realms of Faerie. <br /><br />Years later I would find this was also a favorite of my friend Janice and our late friend Bonnie. We had plans to establish our own Tamson House where we could live separate lives with the support of our friends and create as we each did. Even though the</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2117790675701065296">The book which was my introduction to Charles de Lint. One of the most unforgettable characters in this book is actually a house. Tamson House. A huge rooming house with a center courtyard/garden which opens into the Otherworld realms of Faerie. <br /><br />Years later I would find this was also a favorite of my friend Janice and our late friend Bonnie. We had plans to establish our own Tamson House where we could live separate lives with the support of our friends and create as we each did. Even though the plan faded away after Bonnie left us, it is still an excellent idea for a way of living.<br /><br />As to the rest of the story, it was one I felt immediate kinship with as though I could have found myself written into the pages and not have been surprised.<br /><br />Charles de Lint blends elements of Nat. people's and Celtic mythology, music and magic into his stories. I was surprised by some of the other reviews which described it as a difficult read. It does sound like some never even bothered to get into the story but panned it without actually reading it. It is a very good place to start for de Lint's writings. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_564984737">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/564984737" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/8823926-so-i-read-this-book-today" class="left imgcol" title="So, I Read This Book Today"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1385785384p2/8823926.jpg" alt="So, I Read This Book Today" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/564984737?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 18, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/8823926-so-i-read-this-book-today" class="user" title="So, I Read This Book Today">So, I Read This Book Today</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer564984737">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6093499791197380138">Who among you remembers the first book you read? Or, shall we say the first which made a real impression on you? For me, I grew up on a household that didn't read, and didn't really provide books for a budding bibliophile. So, I did what I could, mostly snitching school books to read from my older cousins. The first I really remember? Being six years old and sneaking my cousin's high school mythology books from her room. The ideas there absolutely fascinated me. Gods and monsters. Far distant la</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6093499791197380138">Who among you remembers the first book you read? Or, shall we say the first which made a real impression on you? For me, I grew up on a household that didn't read, and didn't really provide books for a budding bibliophile. So, I did what I could, mostly snitching school books to read from my older cousins. The first I really remember? Being six years old and sneaking my cousin's high school mythology books from her room. The ideas there absolutely fascinated me. Gods and monsters. Far distant lands with strange languages and customs. I was truly hooked on mythology, fantasy and reading itself. It was an epiphany of massive personal proportions.<br /><br />Back in the middle/late 80's, I was gifted with “Moonheart.” Another epiphany of massive personal proportions. I fell into the story, into it's world of myth and legend, and became an Urban Fantasy fan for life. Moonheart's story was, for it's time, groundbreaking. While most mythology of the time retained the ancient characteristics of other myths and legends, Moonheart brought the stories into the modern day, creating the modern Urban Fantasy genre. Of course, Emma Bull's “War For The Oaks” winner of the Locus Award for Best First Novel , Terri Windling's “The Wood Wife” and Ellen Datlow's various compilations of UF helped cement my love at the time. I spent years collecting all the works I could get my hands on, including a rare, signed copy of de Lint's “The Buffalo Man,” illustrated by Charles Vess, that I cherish. <br /><br />Moonheart is perfect for anyone who wants to study the beginnings of UF, but it is a tremendous story for what it is – a beautifully written tale combining music (another of my passions), fairie, mystical forests, mythical artifacts and beings and layers upon layers of worlds. De Lint is a musician himself, and his writing is a paean to the musical heart of myth and mystery. <br /><br />A living house which straddles two worlds, a cast of characters who I love dearly. Moonheart is a beautiful story I return to over and over again. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6093499791197380138">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5792706112756780782">I am so sorry you grew up in a house like that. I used to get in trouble for reading, too, but it was because I'd be reading in class with a book under my desk instead of paying attention, or I stayed in the house too long and I needed exercise. My parents started reading to me before I could properly talk and started putting adult books in my hands when I was 11. It's so wonderful that you grew up to be a reader despite not having that push and support. <br /><br />You told me this is one of your favorite books, so I'm adding it to my list. <div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_222800885">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/222800885" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2177891-kat-hooper" class="left imgcol" title="Kat  Hooper"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1446176578p2/2177891.jpg" alt="Kat  Hooper" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/222800885?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 21, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2177891-kat-hooper" class="user" title="Kat  Hooper">Kat  Hooper</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2177891-kat-hooper?shelf=audiobook" class="actionLinkLite">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer222800885">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13665988381693594355">ORIGINALLY POSTED AT <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.fantasyliterature.com">Fantasy Literature</a>.<br /><br />Sara and her uncle Jamie live in Tamson House, the old family mansion that takes up a street block in Ottawa. While Sara runs their cluttered curiosity shop, Jamie spends his days studying the arcane and playing host to the eccentrics and homeless people who come and go through Tamson House. Sara and Jamie’s interests collide when Sara discovers an old gold ring that seems to draw her into an ancient past — a past where Welsh and Native American mythology</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13665988381693594355">ORIGINALLY POSTED AT <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.fantasyliterature.com">Fantasy Literature</a>.<br /><br />Sara and her uncle Jamie live in Tamson House, the old family mansion that takes up a street block in Ottawa. While Sara runs their cluttered curiosity shop, Jamie spends his days studying the arcane and playing host to the eccentrics and homeless people who come and go through Tamson House. Sara and Jamie’s interests collide when Sara discovers an old gold ring that seems to draw her into an ancient past — a past where Welsh and Native American mythology comes alive. But not only does the ring pull Sara in, it draws Tamson House, and all its occupants, with it.<br /><br />Moonheart was a truly satisfying read for me. I fell in love with Tamson House — just the idea of a big sprawling mansion that exists in two worlds is enough to fascinate me. Tamson House was my favorite “character” in Moonheart but, as rarely happens, I liked almost all of the characters in this novel. They feel real and alive, with distinct backgrounds, personalities, and motivations. I enjoyed watching them react to their strange situations and interact with each other.<br /><br />Another aspect of Moonheart that works especially well is the mix of the modern and ancient. It doesn’t feel at all unreasonable when Blue is racing his motorcycle through ancient Wales. In many ways, Moonheart reminded me of Robert Holdstock’s Mythago Wood and Lavondyss, two of my favorite fantasy novels. In each of these stories, an old house exists in both the modern world and on the edge of an old dark forest full of myth and legend. I suppose I just can’t resist this type of story and de Lint does it so well.<br /><br />There is plenty of mystery, suspense, and action in Moonheart, and even some terror, too. I was completely enthralled the whole way through as I listened to Paul Michael Garcia superbly narrate Blackstone audio’s version. Moonheart is an enchanting story.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13665988381693594355">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/141589287?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 20, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4793655-ithlilian" class="user" title="Ithlilian">Ithlilian</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a href="/review/list/4793655-ithlilian?shelf=urban-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">urban-fantasy</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer141589287">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17385821482150991538">I know that deLint is classic, and that everyone should like it because of that, but I didn't like this. Yes, it is going to be different from newer urban fantasy, but it wasn't the slow pace that bothers me. I mean, I have read epic fantasy that progresses at a snails pace. It was more the overall feel of the novel. It just felt like it was from a different time, and that I would have had to be alive then to appreciate it. The style just really didn't sit well with me, and of course the plot, c</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17385821482150991538">I know that deLint is classic, and that everyone should like it because of that, but I didn't like this. Yes, it is going to be different from newer urban fantasy, but it wasn't the slow pace that bothers me. I mean, I have read epic fantasy that progresses at a snails pace. It was more the overall feel of the novel. It just felt like it was from a different time, and that I would have had to be alive then to appreciate it. The style just really didn't sit well with me, and of course the plot, characters, and setting weren't that appealing to me either. It would be like reading an urban fantasy twenty years from now that was just published recently. People might not understand the Harry Potter references, the weird abbreviations, and other references to pop culture that are so overused in books now. I understand that all of those things make books relevant to the time, but something was lost in translation here. Not my style.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17385821482150991538">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer16934962708934527322">My first brush with Charles de Lindt, when I was 16. The first time I fell in love with a character in a book! (This was before I met Heathcliffe, but Blue, the biker shaman in Moonheart, knocks the socks even off him!) I love the way that de Lindt moves his stories effortlessly between the *real* world and the *other* worlds that he writes about. If anyone has been there, along with Brian Froud, it's him. He's not as well known in the UK as he is in the US, which is a shame. Should be shelved a</span>

He's not as well known in the UK as he is in the US, which is a shame. Should be shelved alongside Neil Gaiman.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_21479446">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/21479446" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1135341-emily" class="left imgcol" title="Emily"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1209764276p2/1135341.jpg" alt="Emily" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/21479446?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 02, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1135341-emily" class="user" title="Emily">Emily</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer21479446">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13075357514267262845">I read this in college after a friend strongly recommended it. This is easily a world that you can fall into, with believable characters in an unbelievable world. The best way to describe this book is an "urban fantasy," but it's more than that. There are dusty bookshelves, mysterious artifacts, a house that transcends earthly limits, a biker dude named Blue, a computer named Memoria, and Native American rituals. This is a great way to introduce yourself to Charles de Lint.</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/27028288" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/67156-steven" class="left imgcol" title="Steven"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1178726773p2/67156.jpg" alt="Steven" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/27028288?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 12, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/67156-steven" class="user" title="Steven">Steven</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/67156-steven?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer27028288">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8261211117478712305">This is an urban fantasy that delivers on every promise it makes; unlike many of the current crop, this book gives you characters to really care about, as opposed to making the protagonists nearly as unlikable as the antagonists. <br /><br />Well worth the read for any who like Celtic mythology, shamanism, or just a little magic in your everyday. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1121194925?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 04, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/638231-krazykiwi" class="user" title="Krazykiwi">Krazykiwi</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1121194925">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4824716904603805108">It's not perfect, it's a bit rambly, sometimes a little confusing. It's dated in places, and not always too well. The characterisation is a little uneven, and there are almost too many characters to keep track of at times. Even de Lint himself in the afterword admits it's not the book he set out to write, and not the one he'd write now, but he resists the temptation to "fix" it.<br /><br />And I'm giving it 5 stars anyway, because any book that sticks with you for 25 years, and still gives as much joy to re</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4824716904603805108">It's not perfect, it's a bit rambly, sometimes a little confusing. It's dated in places, and not always too well. The characterisation is a little uneven, and there are almost too many characters to keep track of at times. Even de Lint himself in the afterword admits it's not the book he set out to write, and not the one he'd write now, but he resists the temptation to "fix" it.<br /><br />And I'm giving it 5 stars anyway, because any book that sticks with you for 25 years, and still gives as much joy to read now as it did then, has to be worth that. Even just looking at it sitting on my shelf, next to a hardcover of Greenmantle I picked up a couple of years ago, makes me happy. Now I just have to find myself another copy of Faerie Tale.<br /><br />Full review @<a rel="nofollow" href="http://krazykiwi.booklikes.com/post/1056982/moonheart-by-charles-de-lint">Booklikes</a></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_965330825">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/965330825" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/83319-christina" class="left imgcol" title="Christina"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1370462585p2/83319.jpg" alt="Christina" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/965330825?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 16, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/83319-christina" class="user" title="Christina">Christina</a>

          marked it as to-read




        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/83319-christina?shelf=unfinished" class="actionLinkLite">unfinished</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer965330825">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16433122247192749529">I think if I'd tried reading this book in the late nineties, I would have actually made it all the way through. Maybe even liked it. In the nineties, the New Age flavor of this fantasy world would have been less jarring, because Native American appropriation and Celtic mysticism were in style. Also, in the nineties, I was a teenager. I wouldn't have seen anything wrong with the way the main characters were all so quirky they could barely function in the "real world" and how they're portrayed as</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16433122247192749529">I think if I'd tried reading this book in the late nineties, I would have actually made it all the way through. Maybe even liked it. In the nineties, the New Age flavor of this fantasy world would have been less jarring, because Native American appropriation and Celtic mysticism were in style. Also, in the nineties, I was a teenager. I wouldn't have seen anything wrong with the way the main characters were all so quirky they could barely function in the "real world" and how they're portrayed as 100% RIGHT for doing so. I hadn't moved to Boston yet, so I might not have read "Yaqui Way" as "Yawkey Way." I also had teenage self-esteem and would have assumed that it was completely natural that every woman in de Lint's books is described as a waif with delicate bones and huge eyes. I'm always really tickled at those descriptions where the author's like "she wasn't pretty or anything" and then describes a slim, wavy-haired, large-eyed young white woman with clear skin.<br />And gosh, de Lint gave us a heroine who looked like that, dresses like the heroine of Flashdance (but doesn't follow fashion because she has to be real), and is charmingly quirky. She also says "Ooooh!" when frustrated. And she faces down a rude old lady named Geraldine Hathaway who is clearly A Victorian Spinster in the 1980s. <br />I might have eaten all this up in 1997. But time, etc., and now I have to spit it back out.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16433122247192749529">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_968268242">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/968268242" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/102840-grace" class="left imgcol" title="Grace"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1180384648p2/102840.jpg" alt="Grace" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/968268242?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 15, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/102840-grace" class="user" title="Grace">Grace</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer968268242">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7228579587571262297">de Lint is one of my favorite authors, but this early work of his was disappointing to me. Why? 1) he uses names that are unpronounceable. For some reason, I want to be able to pronounce the names in my head, and I hate not being able to do that. The names are filled with apostrophes and other punctuation marks that irritate me. 2) Too many characters. The whole RCMP subplot should have been cut from the book. 3) he has this one character always swearing with these really nasty swears: "Lord lif</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7228579587571262297">de Lint is one of my favorite authors, but this early work of his was disappointing to me. Why? 1) he uses names that are unpronounceable. For some reason, I want to be able to pronounce the names in my head, and I hate not being able to do that. The names are filled with apostrophes and other punctuation marks that irritate me. 2) Too many characters. The whole RCMP subplot should have been cut from the book. 3) he has this one character always swearing with these really nasty swears: "Lord lifting Jesus" (what on earth is that, anyway) and "Lord dying Jesus." He did it so often that it got to be really distracting and it would pull me out of the narrative to fulminate about the swear every time it happened. Not to mention that as a Christian, I found it incredibly offensive. <br /><br />Plot: girl finds some strange Indian artifacts, lives in house with a soul, evil is afoot, girl and guy go to alternate dimension to stop the evil. Add about 100 characters to this with about half of them having unpronounceable names, and there you have it. <br /><br />This book won't put me off de Lint for good, but I'm not rushing out to get the next in his list, either. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7228579587571262297">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13729571251758820530">The 'swear' terms were very French Canadian, where most swear terms/oaths are religion based. The way Kieran swore was very true to Quebecois swearing. English people, however, use scatological or sex terms, which I'm sure people in Quebec consider really gross.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_125329762">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/125329762" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3725822-stephen" class="left imgcol" title="Stephen"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1299004045p2/3725822.jpg" alt="Stephen" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/125329762?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 18, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3725822-stephen" class="user" title="Stephen">Stephen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3725822-stephen?shelf=urban-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">urban-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer125329762">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6611663648754918670">For years I have heard great things about Moonheart. So much hype centers around this as a defining work in the career of Charles de Lint. I had read several works by de Lint and liked them all. However, all the hype made it hard for me to get started and this book sat on my shelf for a year.<br /><br />Then I started reading. The book starts in 'real world' Ottawa with introductions for some of the recurring 'main' characters. This part moved nicely along and set up the blend of mystery, urban fantasy, and</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6611663648754918670">For years I have heard great things about Moonheart. So much hype centers around this as a defining work in the career of Charles de Lint. I had read several works by de Lint and liked them all. However, all the hype made it hard for me to get started and this book sat on my shelf for a year.<br /><br />Then I started reading. The book starts in 'real world' Ottawa with introductions for some of the recurring 'main' characters. This part moved nicely along and set up the blend of mystery, urban fantasy, and folklore that I had been told about. I was pulled into the story of mages, Paranormal Research Branch agents, and Celtic myths awakened in present day Canada. Then things slow down as a landslide of characters are introduced.<br /><br />There are a lot of characters in this book! Many are featured in their own sub-plots. Sometimes the switching from one character's story to anothers gets really disconcerting and makes for a really tough read. I felt overwhelmed and had to take it very slowly through the middle of the book (about pages 150-350). <br /><br />Then, suddenly the action ratchets up around page 350 and all the characters begin to weave together into the primary plot line. The great story that has been hyped suddenly presents itself over the final 80-90 pages.<br /><br />Overall, I liked the story and enjoyed the weaving of fantasy and modern mystery. I was most impressed by the development of the Tamson House as a character (a house that is a living being straddling several worlds while sitting downtown Ottawa). As one of the fore-runners of today's Urban Fantasy, this is a great book in many ways. I still like de Lint's earlier works better(Riddle of the Wren, Harp of the Grey Rose). <br /><br />As the book is split into 4 parts, I will rate the parts first.<br /><br />Part 1 and Part 4 - 4.5 Stars<br />Part 2 and Part 3 - 2.0 Stars<br />Overall - 3.0 Stars<br /><br />If you like a fast pace and lots of action, you may want to skip this one. If you like Urban Fantasy and want to see one of the original stories that got it kick-started as a genre, then read this. If you are a fan of Celtic myth, nature magic, and other-dimension exploration, read this book. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6611663648754918670">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <a href="/user/show/3623192-cheryl" class="user" title="Cheryl">Cheryl</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3623192-cheryl?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3623192-cheryl?shelf=contemporary-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">contemporary-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer162521024">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14686945356120515244">Charles de Lint is one of my favourite authors of contemporary fantasy. He makes me believe in magic and that it could be alive today. I was so excited to find this gem in my local second-hand bookstore. <br /><br />The combination of ancient lore and modern times works in this book. Written in 1984, except for a couple of references to obsolete technology, it stands the test of time. Sara Kendell finds a medicine bag and a remarkably clear picture of two men in the back of her uncle’s store. She and her un</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14686945356120515244">Charles de Lint is one of my favourite authors of contemporary fantasy. He makes me believe in magic and that it could be alive today. I was so excited to find this gem in my local second-hand bookstore. <br /><br />The combination of ancient lore and modern times works in this book. Written in 1984, except for a couple of references to obsolete technology, it stands the test of time. Sara Kendell finds a medicine bag and a remarkably clear picture of two men in the back of her uncle’s store. She and her uncle, Jamie Tams, live in Tamson House, a large and magical home, located in Ottawa. They are independently wealthy but open this house to an eclectic group of misfits passing through our nation’s capital. Around the same time, Kieran Foy arrives in Ottawa looking for his magical mentor whose bond to him disappeared recently. This magical mentor, Tom Hengwr, is not to be found but we do discover he is looking for the bard, Taliesin. Taliesin is one of the men in the picture that Sara found. In searching for the origins of the medicine bag, she and Kieran are attacked by dark forces and taken to the otherworld. The quest to fight the darkness and protect the ones they love is an engrossing read. <br /><br />Themes of love and the conflict of good and evil in all of us are clearly portrayed. We must remember that this is one of de Lint’s earliest novels, as such, it is a bit gauche. However, he draws on the spirituality of native Americans and contrasts it with Arthurian legends, placing a plausible link to Ottawa and the Otherworld. My favourite character in the book is Tamson House itself, followed closely by Sara. Some of the other characters were a bit hot and cold, sometimes feeling real but in other places behaving very statically. Overall, I enjoyed the book and read it relatively quickly over the Easter holiday, so any negative comments are just picky points from a reader who is more familiar with de Lint’s recent work.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14686945356120515244">...more</a>

          </span>

      <a href="/user/show/2298109-denis" class="user" title="Denis">Denis</a>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer782196708">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2671914753350106464">I am not a regular reader of Fantasy (though I have read Heinlein's "Glory Road" and "The Hobbit...") I admit, I am a bit of a snob about this genre. My step daughter Shaela defends and debates, all in fun of course, it with me all the time. I say it's all Goblins, Unicorns, Elves, Wizards, Dragons... and plots based on a group of people along with Trolls, Ogres (or Oafs...) and so on, walking around in the woods with bows and arrows, swords or sling shots in search of some kind of trinket or an</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2671914753350106464">I am not a regular reader of Fantasy (though I have read Heinlein's "Glory Road" and "The Hobbit...") I admit, I am a bit of a snob about this genre. My step daughter Shaela defends and debates, all in fun of course, it with me all the time. I say it's all Goblins, Unicorns, Elves, Wizards, Dragons... and plots based on a group of people along with Trolls, Ogres (or Oafs...) and so on, walking around in the woods with bows and arrows, swords or sling shots in search of some kind of trinket or another that will liberate... and when the author gets to a point where he can't explain why this or that is possible, he then simply solve it by introducing magic which is but the convenient tool used to explain the unexplainable; the implausible. <br /><br />Shaela defends her favorite genre by pointing out that the characters are colourful, strong, empowering... and I do agree that the genre is the truest for escapism (if escapism is why you read). And I've heard the argument a hundred times that spaceships and aliens are no more real than Fairies and Sorceresses... Okay, fair enough.<br /><br />Shaela liked, in particular the novel, "The Onion Girl" by this author, so I thought I'd look into him and found that this author de Lint writes fantasy in a way I could accept and enjoy. It is a sub-genre entitled "Urban Fantasy", which seems to mean that it is more grounded within the real world rather than the fictitious. My own world at that: The setting is Ottawa, Ontario (I am from Ontario). From there, real characters are developed and then things get all mixed up in the fantasy worlds with a strange mix of Welsh mythology and North American aboriginal myth and yes, magic but, the way it was written, overall, it worked and I liked it just fine (you hear that Shaela?). As well as this being typically a fantasy novel, it had elements of mystery, SF and drama. It was even a bit like reading Stephen King.<br /></span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer163476947">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13883155051552182940">I had read de Lint's <em>The Riddle of the Wren</em> years ago - around 1980, if I recall - and remember nothing about it except the cool cover, with its Celtic-influenced design. <br /><br />Then a friend passed <em>Moonheart</em> to me as a must-read, and I was hooked.<br /><br />De Lint's storytelling web is woven out of European folk beliefs, Celtic myth, Native American practice, that little niggle inside your head telling you there's more to certain places than meets the eye, mysterious pockets of forest amid an urban setting that</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13883155051552182940">I had read de Lint's <em>The Riddle of the Wren</em> years ago - around 1980, if I recall - and remember nothing about it except the cool cover, with its Celtic-influenced design. <br /><br />Then a friend passed <em>Moonheart</em> to me as a must-read, and I was hooked.<br /><br />De Lint's storytelling web is woven out of European folk beliefs, Celtic myth, Native American practice, that little niggle inside your head telling you there's more to certain places than meets the eye, mysterious pockets of forest amid an urban setting that feel like they could easily be home to a band of faery, those recurring dreams where you find a hidden door in your house that leads to a previously unknown treasure trove right next door... Somehow it avoids becoming a hodge-podge, and draws you irresistably into his spell.<br /><br />I subsequently have read everything de Lint has written - up to a point about 5 years ago, when I got behind and haven't yet caught up: the man is ridiculously prolific. The earlier books, like <em>Harp of the Grey Rose</em> &amp; <em>Riddle</em>, aren't nearly as good, IMO: seemed like he was trying to hew too closely to European traditions. <em>Greenmantle</em> was (I believe) the first that brought those old stories into a modern setting, and once he hit on that combination, his unique voice began to come through loud and clear. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13883155051552182940">...more</a>

          </span>

      <a href="/user/show/7565775-donovan" class="user" title="Donovan">Donovan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer319684930">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15566196740633850024">Overall, I had a connection with Moonheart. I read it in the early 90's and something within it struck me and I know I liked it. Unfortunately the novel reads like a dream and as with a dream it quickly becomes forgotten. I will re-read it as this dream-like quality is typical of Charles de Lint's work. That does NOT mean it's terribly written. On the contrary, it is elegant in many ways.<br />The pace is slow though, so some may find it boring. There are a lot of subtleties that have to do with the p</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15566196740633850024">Overall, I had a connection with Moonheart. I read it in the early 90's and something within it struck me and I know I liked it. Unfortunately the novel reads like a dream and as with a dream it quickly becomes forgotten. I will re-read it as this dream-like quality is typical of Charles de Lint's work. That does NOT mean it's terribly written. On the contrary, it is elegant in many ways.<br />The pace is slow though, so some may find it boring. There are a lot of subtleties that have to do with the pagan influence and many of these could be lost on the average reader - which is a shame as there has been a lot of research undertaken.<br />I recommend Moonheart for any dreamy reader who find themselves stuck in the real world and fantasises over the romantic tales of the Fey.<br /><br />Plot ***Spoilers***<br />Moonheart takes place in 1980s Ottawa, where Sara Kendell and Jamie Tamson, owners of an antique store, come into possession of a peculiar ring. At the same time Kieran Foy, a wizard of sorts, is searching for his missing mentor, Thomas Hengwr, while simultaneously eluding the newly formed Project Mindreach from the RCMP. When Sara and Kieran run into each other and end up in "the other world," they find that there is much more to Sara and to the Tamson house (Sara and Jamie's house the size of a city block) than previously known. But when an ancient evil resurfaces and threatens to destroy both worlds (and all the people within), the officers, wizards, and Tamson house residents have to learn to work together to destroy the monster. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15566196740633850024">...more</a>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/624252349" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1188122-ben-babcock" class="left imgcol" title="Ben Babcock"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1211839871p2/1188122.jpg" alt="Ben Babcock" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/624252349?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 09, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1188122-ben-babcock" class="user" title="Ben Babcock">Ben Babcock</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/1870632.Moonheart" title="Paperback 9780330315760" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1188122-ben-babcock?shelf=own" class="actionLinkLite">own</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1188122-ben-babcock?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1188122-ben-babcock?shelf=canadian-author" class="actionLinkLite">canadian-author</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1188122-ben-babcock?shelf=romance" class="actionLinkLite">romance</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1188122-ben-babcock?shelf=urban-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">urban-fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1188122-ben-babcock?shelf=2013-read" class="actionLinkLite">2013-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer624252349">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2946393909865971182">I’m Canadian and a lover of fantasy but have somehow managed not to read any books by Charles de Lint, ever. I’m not sure if this represents great skill or just gobsmacking stupidity on my part. It’s probably some mixture of the two. My roommate lent me <em>Moonheart</em>, promising I would love it in tones that made me hope I would, lest awkward differences of critical opinion ensue. Fortunately, I do love this book. De Lint does an amazing job combining Celtic mythology and contemporary Canada to creat</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2946393909865971182">I’m Canadian and a lover of fantasy but have somehow managed not to read any books by Charles de Lint, ever. I’m not sure if this represents great skill or just gobsmacking stupidity on my part. It’s probably some mixture of the two. My roommate lent me <em>Moonheart</em>, promising I would love it in tones that made me hope I would, lest awkward differences of critical opinion ensue. Fortunately, I do love this book. De Lint does an amazing job combining Celtic mythology and contemporary Canada to create a compelling urban fantasy.<br /><br /><em>Moonheart</em> starts off slow. De Lint takes his time introducing the large cast of characters. We meet Sara Kendell and her uncle, Jamie Tamson (aka Tams). They are rich—and not just wealthy, but super-rich. (I assume that de Lint did this in order to justify why Jamie has such an awesome house, but it’s fun to watch him twist himself into knots justifying how Jamie and Sara can be such down-to-earth people despite their insane wealth.) We meet Kieran Foy and his mentor in all-things-magery, Tom Hengwr. We meet the honourable but hard Inspector Tucker, an RCMP officer.<br /><br />Did I mention it’s set in Canada? Ottawa, to be precise. I’ve never actually been (I hear it’s nice, though), so de Lint’s descriptions of the streets and neighbourhoods didn’t jog any fond memories as I’m sure they would for some people. Nevertheless, there’s just something so … Canadian … about the way he describes our capital city. It’s nice to see it featured in so good a novel. And it’s nice to see the RCMP grappling with the possibilities of paranormal threats instead of leaving it to the FBI and the CIA.<br /><br />Also, I wasn’t aware of how old the book was until the conspicuous lack of references to cell phones, the Internet, and the paucity of computers in general drove me to look at the copyright page: 1984! <em>Moonheart</em> holds up really well, though, because de Lint has crafted a story that’s perfect for its time. Although it is possible to write great urban fantasy set in a twenty-first century city, the motifs and tropes that one embraces will be different. I don’t think it would alter de Lint’s grand theme about the inevitable changes in human society, but the way he would deal with those changes would be different, and even perhaps more obvious. By dint of its time, <em>Moonheart</em> has more breathing room: the Cold War is over, but the frenetic digital era has yet to take off.<br /><br />This is reflected in the pacing of the novel. As I mentioned above, it takes a while for <em>Moonheart</em>’s conflict to get going. This works, though, because de Lint’s writing is good enough to keep the reader interested. There are few outright boring scenes in this book; no matter which group of characters we’re with, something interesting is happening. Though there is a fair amount of dialogue, de Lint has the ability to seamlessly scatter exposition and description within a conversation. All in all, reading <em>Moonheart</em> is a pleasant and effortless experience that belies the complexity of what’s actually going on.<br /><br />I mean, let’s step back for a moment: the “mundane” characters (for wont of a better term) in this novel stumble into the middle of a 1500-year-old feud between a Celtic bard (who is ostensibly dead) and a druid (who is now just a crabby old man, because that is the fate of all of us). It is actually more complicated than this, for reasons I can’t go into because SPOILERS. Along the way, the characters learn that magic is a) very real and b) not actually all that fun. In this universe, magic—at least for humans and certain types of creatures who seem to be related to or have human ancestors—is all about centering oneself and having inner calm. And <em>then</em> you can blast people with fire.<br /><br />Kieran and Sara meet the aforementioned bard, Taleisin, and Sara falls in love. She engages in some time-travelling shenanigans that probably make things worse, before becoming relevant again just in time to participate in the climax of the novel. If I have to lob any criticism vaguely in <em>Moonheart</em>’s direction, it’s Sara’s role and development. Don’t get the wrong idea: like all of the main characters, Sara changes throughout this novel, and de Lint spends a good amount of time depicting it. Compared to the other characters, however, she seems to have the least amount of page-time where she actually does something—there is a lot of waiting and complaining going on. This is a shame, particularly since she’s the only major female character in the book, and her role is connected to the title.<br /><br />Meanwhile, Jamie, Tucker, and a biker named Blue find themselves trapped in Tamson House with an assortment of other characters of various loyalties. Not only is the house under siege by mysterious, wolverine-like shadow creatures, but it has hopped its interior into an alternative dimension. (Yes, it’s the kind of magical, semi-sentient house that every fantasy book needs and most fantasy readers would want. It is awesome.) They have to fend off this assault, figure out how the house got into this dimension and how to get back, and deal with internal strife. Their only source of information is a comatose, healing Tom Hengwr, who certainly knows more than even he is able to say. As all this happens, an external enemy lies in wait, looking for a way into the house to kill Tom and everyone inside (but especially Tom).<br /><br />In this way, <em>Moonheart</em> is both an intense action novel and a mystery as well. The characters (and to some extent the reader) have to piece together how these various, almost disparate myths and stories relate to what actually happened so many centuries ago. I know almost nothing about Celtic mythology, so I’m can’t speak to how accurately or well de Lint represents it here. But I think he uses it to good effect. He embraces the convention in fantasy that the old gods and old magic have faded in proportion to humanity’s belief in them fading; elves and manitou and related spirits have withdrawn from our world into the Otherworlds as humanity turns to science and technology and away from nature and mythology. Unlike some novels, though, <em>Moonheart</em> does not view this as depressing; it just <em>is</em>, and there is no point in complaining.<br /><br />De Lint hints that the time of even wizards is drawing to a close, that soon magic in general will be gone from our world. He doesn’t explore this as fully as he might; <em>Moonheart</em> ends with the surviving characters changed irrecovably, but the extent of how those changes affect what they do with the rest of their lives is an open question. I definitely get the sense, however, a major point de Lint makes here is how the battles one fights always change one, and those changes are usually unforeseen. None of the major characters is the same by the end of the book. Some of them have undergone major transformations, while others have merely (<em>merely</em>) had their world-views altered. Regardless, <em>Moonheart</em> emphasizes how life is never static; by definition, experiences—and particularly conflict—force us to make choices about who we will be. Will we fight with honour? Will we let pride be our undoing? Will we embrace what we see or deny that it is happening?<br /><br />So, in addition to its action aspects, <em>Moonheart</em> is an excellent fantasy novel. De Lint balances mythology and magic with the novel’s modern-day setting. It’s been a long time since I have read such a nice, original urban fantasy story. It reminds me a little of <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/56250629">
  <em>Faerie Tale</em>
</a>, but it’s never quite as dark. The two books are similar in that both are set strongly in this world but also have links to other, more fey worlds, and the characters’ discovery of the reality of magic and magical creatures gets them into deep trouble.<br /><br />If you like fantasy, read <em>Moonheart</em>. It’s as simple as that. I can’t guarantee you’ll like the characters, the plot, or the story quite as much as me. But de Lint’s skill as a writer, combined with this story, are more than enough to make me sing this novel’s praises.<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/">
  <img width="88" height="31" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380238653i/681550.png" alt="Creative Commons BY-NC License" /></a></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2946393909865971182">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10243173827006955962">Moonheart has a fascinating premiss. Using Celtic and Native American mythology together is, at least to my knowledge, unique even in the genre of fantasy. <br /><br />Which is why I gave this book two stars instead of one; flat, stereotyped characters and stilted dialogue made this book really difficult to get through. <br /><br />For all of the time the author spends explaining his major characters to us, for all the lengthy descriptions of backstory in scenes where the information is completely irrelevant, not a sin</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10243173827006955962">Moonheart has a fascinating premiss. Using Celtic and Native American mythology together is, at least to my knowledge, unique even in the genre of fantasy. <br /><br />Which is why I gave this book two stars instead of one; flat, stereotyped characters and stilted dialogue made this book really difficult to get through. <br /><br />For all of the time the author spends explaining his major characters to us, for all the lengthy descriptions of backstory in scenes where the information is completely irrelevant, not a single one of his characters feels genuine or compelling. They feel two-dimensional and sometimes painfully stereotyped (eg. Keiran's rastafarian friend, whose dialogue had me cringing). Interactions between characters felt forced and inconsistent, and as a result, it was extremely difficult for me to care about plot or setting, the book's only real strengths. <br /><br />I wanted to like this book. I really tried to. But it fell very short of my expectations and I wouldn't recommend it. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10243173827006955962">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_892531993">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/892531993" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5716269-zachary" class="left imgcol" title="Zachary"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1309417761p2/5716269.jpg" alt="Zachary" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/892531993?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 30, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5716269-zachary" class="user" title="Zachary">Zachary</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer892531993">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16214136589472970682">There is something special about this book, or perhaps its author. It conveys a sense of wonder and a mingling of the ordinary and the fantastic with ease, despite some problematic characterization and a pace which is at times frustrating.<br /><br />It is the story of Sara Kendell, an ordinary young woman from Ottawa, and her encounter with a mysterious bunch of artifacts one day in her uncle's antique store. She and her friends will embark on a journey to another world, meet with figures drawn from mythol</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16214136589472970682">There is something special about this book, or perhaps its author. It conveys a sense of wonder and a mingling of the ordinary and the fantastic with ease, despite some problematic characterization and a pace which is at times frustrating.<br /><br />It is the story of Sara Kendell, an ordinary young woman from Ottawa, and her encounter with a mysterious bunch of artifacts one day in her uncle's antique store. She and her friends will embark on a journey to another world, meet with figures drawn from mythology, and confront evil in classic fantasy fashion. The book does a wonderful job of drawing these characters and their surroundings realistically, including the strange house that they call home, which is in some ways a character in itself.<br /><br />The problematic aspects mostly surround the book's portrayal of Native Americans, which tends to cleave to the "magical Indian," trope with unfortunate dogmatism. It isn't done maliciously, but might be a little jarring if the reader isn't expecting it. The characters make sense in context, but the underlying trope is still present, and should be examined further. The pacing problems are more subtle: there is a lot of exposition at the front of the book, and some throughout the rest, which can be slow going. Sometimes the book lingered on characters a little too long, I felt, or left us waiting for them to reappear for more time than was necessary.<br /><br />These fairly minor quibbles aside, I must reemphasize that this is a wonderful book. There are moments of great joy to be found here, and the conclusion is satisfying and worth the journey. I hardily recommend the read to anybody with an open mind and a desire for something out of the ordinary.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16214136589472970682">...more</a>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;name=' + shelf_name + '&amp;a=' + action + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('lm1IvwFYyaUt3xVZIpOHFWZO2NOg5gCFQjXvsNqAuMjzuXds84rdX6EHYeJc8mGnL7kbyuk7Ry8oPOoiFhVOjQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&amp;book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;refresh=true' + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('FFAStLD15bgQJG3HlFXvw14ehjtj0aga3Rf2+szvhH9xhC1nQifxQpz8GXzqNAlxF+lFIioM77C3HvNoAHpyOg==')})
  }

And so he kept himself on the outside, watching, noticing, and sniffing, most often in the company of those who bore watching. Among them, flamboyant, mystical curate Charlie Iredale; outrageous banker Darcy Dwyer; cynical, quixotic professor Brocky Gilmartin, whose son Conor, also Hullah\'s godson, makes a fateful and too brief appearance in Robertson Davies\'s last novel, Murther &amp;amp; Walking Spirits. Hullah also lives in close proximity to Pansy Freake Todhunter, an etcher in Toronto. Indeed he becomes privy to her intimate letters to British sculptor Barbara Hepworth. It is &amp;quot;Chips,&amp;quot; as she is called, who writes Dame Barbara: &amp;quot;The doctor is a bit of a puzzle. Just as Dr. Hullah practices a type of psychosomatic medicine &amp;quot;by which I attempt to bring about changes in the disease syndromes through language,&amp;quot; so does Robertson Davies
